Rational we are not.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For example, a tax incorporated into a price tag on supermarket shelves makes us more frugal, whereas an equivalent tax added at checkout is virtually ignored. We also spend the same money differently depending on whether it&rsquo;s labelled credit, rebate, or bonus, and our willingness to pay for stuff is heavily influenced by the payment method. One recent article listed &lt;a href=\"http://www.theatlantic.com/business/archive/2012/07/the-11-ways-that-consumers-are-hopeless-at-math/259479/\"&gt;eleven ways in which consumers behave irrationally&lt;/a&gt; when confronted with maths. There are probably more. When it comes to moo-la, the technical term for us is &ldquo;cuckoo&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Economic impact&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Inconsistencies born from biases and tricks of the mind are one thing; superstition is another. The idea of a &ldquo;Friday 13th effect&rdquo; came to prominence thanks to a &lt;a href=\"http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1111/j.1540-6261.1987.tb04373.x/abstract\"&gt;1987 study by Robert Kolb and Ricardo Rodriguez&lt;/a&gt;, two finance professors at the &lt;a href=\"http://www.csmonitor.com/tags/topic/University+of+Miami\"&gt;University of Miami&lt;/a&gt;, who found that you are more likely to lose in the stock market on that day, as opposed to a &ldquo;normal&rdquo; Friday. Their findings have since been disputed by &lt;a href=\"http://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=234828&amp;http://www.google.co.uk/url?sa=t&amp;rct=j&amp;q=friday%2013th%20effect%201987%20%20robert%20kolb%20and%20ricardo%20rodriguez%2C&amp;source=web&amp;cd=4&amp;sqi=2&amp;ved=0CG0QFjAD&amp;url=http%3A%2F%2Fpapers.ssrn.com%2Fsol3%25\"&gt;other&lt;/a&gt; &lt;a href=\"http://www.google.co.uk/url?sa=t&amp;rct=j&amp;q=recent%20evidence%20on%20friday%20the%20thirteenth%20effect%20in%20u.s.%20stock%20returns&amp;source=web&amp;cd=1&amp;ved=0CGgQFjAA&amp;url=http%3A%2F%2Fjournals.cluteonline.com%2Findex.php%2FJBER%2Farticle%2Fview%2F2271&amp;ei=_jEWUN\"&gt;stock market&lt;/a&gt; studies, but Kolb and Rodriguez say the paper was published tongue-in-cheek. Their point was not that the day itself had an effect, it was to illustrate how superstition has an effect on our behaviour.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;These beliefs have a massive, and largely negative, impact on the economy &ndash;boom times for fortune tellers notwithstanding. Chung has estimated that Friday the 13th costs the US alone hundreds of millions of dollars, due not only to all those elevators and buildings indicating the absence of a 13th floor, but also lost revenue due to shortened vacations and purchases bypassed because of that unlucky date. Realtors in Hong Kong have reported that apartments deemed haunted might sell for as little as 40% of what a comparable un-haunted apartment might bring in.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;According to a recent Wall Street Journal article, the majority of stock traders in Shanghai are not bankers pursuing value based on market analysis, but everyday individuals who &ldquo;gamble on numerical superstitions, with little understanding of concepts from the financial arena.&rdquo; Not that those who have mastered said &ldquo;concepts&rdquo; have necessarily done great things for anyone of late. &ldquo;Given the failure of all these sophisticated mathematical models that have turned geniuses into morons over the years,&rdquo; says Marc Hochstein, executive editor of the financial services daily American Banker, &ldquo;it&rsquo;ll be interesting to see how a model [like Sid] that intentionally has no rhyme or reason performs.&rdquo;&lt;strong&gt; &lt;br /&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Three years ago, a finance professor in Copenhagen looked at 80 years worth of data from the New York Stock Exchange, S&amp;P 500, the Dow Jones Composite Average, and the Dow Jones Industrial Average, and tried to determine whether the 362 solar and lunar eclipses that took place over that time period affected investment decisions and the economy. &ldquo;[T]he stock market is an ideal breeding ground for superstition,&rdquo; &lt;a href=\"http://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=1428792\"&gt;wrote Gabriele Lepori&lt;/a&gt;, who found below-average returns during times of &ldquo;negative superstitious events&rdquo;, like eclipses.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;His and other researchers&rsquo; findings suggest that we resort more heavily to superstition when there is high uncertainty, the stakes are high, and when we perceive outcomes to be essentially beyond our control. The great irony, of course, is that superstitions are supposed to protect us from misfortune. Yet they do the opposite. They are expensive, and their cost is borne by believers and eye-rollers alike.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Unproven experiment&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For Chung, the whole thing was personal at first but it quickly snowballed. He comes from a family that subscribes to many superstitions. His mother never orders seven dishes at a restaurant (unlucky number) and has filled the house with lucky bamboo and statues of the Buddha holding gold (lucky vegetation, deity, and metal respectively). More recently, she decided at the last minute not to buy a house because the feng shui of the place was off; a decision made because of the direction the toilet faced.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;After maintaining a blog about superstition for a while, Chung decided that a fund that actually trades on the FTSE100 would be an interesting way to explore the murky intersection of value, faith and the economy. &ldquo;Where money and risk are involved,&rdquo; he says, &ldquo;superstition is there lurking in the background&rdquo; &ndash; at casinos, of course, but also on Wall Street. &ldquo;I wanted to poke fun at our superstitions,&rdquo; he says, &ldquo;but I also wanted to highlight our irrational nature in a way that is less critical and more about encouraging dialogue.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;At present he plans to let the fund run for a year, tracking its performance against various indices and other funds, both managed and automated. Should there be gains, he will send dividends to people like me who threw down for a stake in this otherworldly approach to playing the markets. (Yes, his mother is an investor, to the tune of &pound;100/$157) If the whole thing tanks, the money is gone. &lt;em&gt;Poof&lt;/em&gt;. But at least investors are aware of that risk ahead of time, not to mention reminded of it in every correspondence from Chung, which now includes the cautionary e-mail tag: &lt;em&gt;Please be aware, that this is an unproven, highly speculative, experiment and a project with the risk of total loss.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Meanwhile, everyone and their cousin is reaching out to him to share titbits about their personal superstitions, and he is currently making the rounds to talk with future-of-money thinkers in London, audiences at Microsoft, CNBC, CNET, The Wall Street Journal, among others.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Has the whole experience made him want a career in finance? &ldquo;Definitely not,&rdquo; says Chung; he finds the prospect of losing other peoples&rsquo; money quite worrisome. After three weeks of trading the fund had dropped 12% from its starting value of &pound;5,000, while the NASDAQ100 and Hang Seng Index rose around 4% during this period. It has since rebounded a little; at the time of writing the fund is around -5% of its starting amount.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Is losing 5% of value horrible? Depends whom you ask and what you compare it to. In the age of Libor, Lehman, Madoff, and sovereign debt write-offs, maybe it&rsquo;s not so bad. I guess I&rsquo;ll find out next spring, and BBC Future will follow up with Chung to find out how both he and the fund have fared over the course of this experiment. Besides, speculation is just that: not a sure bet. &ldquo;When you gamble,&rdquo; says Chung, &ldquo;you either win or lose.&rdquo; When you put it that way, maybe the more rewarding way to lose $200 would have been an impromptu trip to Las Vegas. Then again, I&rsquo;m a Cancer, and Cancers aren&rsquo;t prone to such rash behaviour.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2012-08-01T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Superstitious Fund: Too mystic to fail","HeadlineShort":"Trading on superstition","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Would you invest in a fund that trades on superstition? David Wolman has, and in the process he discovers unsettling truths about how we treat and handle money.\n","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"Would you trust your money to a robot that invests based on lunar cycles? David Wolman has, and it has led him to discover some unsettling truths about money.","SummaryShort":"Would you trust your money to a robot that invests based on lunar cycles?","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:07Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"fcfff25f-33d8-4a41-9e82-6a5ea61541ce","Id":"wwfuture/story/20120731-bulls-bears-and-black-cats","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:07Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20120731-bulls-bears-and-black-cats"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20120731-bulls-bears-and-black-cats","_id":"559a55f60b808738c4e2eff7"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;The allure and tension of an auction are familiar to most of us &ndash; let&rsquo;s face it, we all like the idea of picking up a bargain. And on-line auction sites like eBay cater for this, allowing us to share in the over-excitement of auction bidding in the privacy of our homes. Yet somehow, despite our better judgement, we end up paying more than we know we should have done on that piece of furniture, equipment or clothing. What's going on?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One estimate states that about half of eBay auctions &lt;a href=\"http://www.nber.org/papers/w13699\"&gt;result in higher sale prices&lt;/a&gt; than the \"buy it now\" price. This is a paradox. If the people going into the auction really wanted the item so badly, why didn't they get it for less by paying the \"buy it now\" price?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This has nothing to do with the way the eBay bidding system works. In fact, unlike most auctions, the eBay auction process is actually &lt;a href=\"http://www.st.ewi.tudelft.nl/%7Emathijs/publications/machazine07.pdf\"&gt;perfectly designed to allow rational outcomes&lt;/a&gt;. By allowing you to set a private &ldquo;maximum bid&rdquo; in advance, eBay auctions are better for individual buyers than public auctions where everyone has to shout out their bid in public. No, the reason auctions &ndash; both on and offline &ndash; produce higher sale prices than any bidder originally imagined they would pay is because of one irreducibly irrational part of the bidding process: the bidders themselves.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Auctions push a number of our psychological buttons, and in fact the phenomenon of &ldquo;&lt;a href=\"http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0749597804000925\"&gt;auction fever&lt;/a&gt;&rdquo; is well documented. They are social occasions, with lots of other people around, and this tends to increase your physiological arousal, an effect called &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_facilitation\"&gt;social facilitation&lt;/a&gt;. As your adrenaline pumps, your heart beats faster, and your reactions quicken. This is ideal for something like sports, but makes cool rational decision making harder. The very rich often send delegates to auctions, and as well as avoiding the paparazzi I suspect this is also a strategy to combat the over-excitement induced by being physically present in the situation.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;On top of this, auctions are time pressured, and &ndash; by definition &ndash; you're bidding on something you value highly. These factors create excitement whether you are in the room or not.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Persuasive powers&lt;/strong&gt;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Another psychological bias that operates in auctions is the &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20120717-why-we-love-to-hoard\"&gt;endowment effect&lt;/a&gt;, where we tend to over-value things we already possess. By encouraging us to connect the bid (our money) with the sale item, bidding on items lets us fantasise about owning them &ndash; stimulating a kind of endowment effect. This is why the auction catalogue (or the item picture and description on a website) is so important. This forms part of the psychological journey the seller wants you to go on to imagine owning this item in advance, so you'll place a higher value on it, and so pay more to make imagination reality.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Persuasion plays a huge part here, and the best book you can read on the psychology of the subject is &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_Cialdini\"&gt;Robert Cialdini's&lt;/a&gt; Influence. Cialdini is a Professor of Social Psychology at Arizona State University, and he lists six major ways you can make yourself persuasive. Auctions hit at least two of these six principles square on the nose.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;First, auctions use the principle of scarcity, whereby we overvalue things that we think might run out. Auction items are scarce in that they are unique (only one person can have it), and scarce in time (after the bids are finished, you've lost your chance). Think how many shop sales successfully rely on scarcity heuristics such as \"Last day of sale!\", or \"Only 2 left in stock!\", and you'll get a feel for how powerful this persuasion principle can be.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The other principle used by auctions is that of &ldquo;social proof&rdquo;. We all tend to take the lead from other people; if everybody does something, or says something, most of us join in before we think about what we really should do. Auctions put you in intimate contact with other people who are all providing social proof that the sale item is important and valuable.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;A final ingredient in the magic-spell cast by auctions was uncovered &lt;a href=\"https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/20305741\"&gt;by researchers from Princeton&lt;/a&gt;. Their experiments asked volunteers to play on-line auctions with different rules. Some of these auctions had rules that encouraged over-bidding (like typical open auctions, which most of us are familiar with from movies), and some had rules that encouraged rational behaviour (like the eBay structure). With enough guidance from the auction rules, the bidders didn't end up paying much more than they originally thought was reasonable - but only if they thought they were bidding against a computer programme. As soon as the volunteers thought they were bidding against other live humans they found it impossible to bid rationally, whatever the auction rules.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This implies that the competitive element of auctions is crucial to provoking our irrational buying behaviour. Once we're involved in an auction we're not just paying to own the sale item, we're paying to beat other people who are bidding and prevent them from having it.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So it seems Gore Vidal had human nature, and the psychology of auctions, about right when he said: \"It is not enough to succeed. Others must fail.\"&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2012-09-11T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Why do we pay more than we should at auctions?","HeadlineShort":"Why we overpay at auctions ","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"The reason we end up overspending is a result of one unavoidably irrational part of the bidding process  and thats ourselves. \n","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"The reason we end up overspending is a result of one unavoidably irrational part of the bidding process  and thats ourselves. ","SummaryShort":"Why bidding battles push all our buttons","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:17Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"98e03252-9ffb-4ed8-88bf-d82136d60c7c","Id":"wwfuture/story/20120907-why-we-overpay-at-auctions","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:17Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20120907-why-we-overpay-at-auctions"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20120907-why-we-overpay-at-auctions","_id":"559a55f90b808738c4e2f05d"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":null,"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;If you type &ldquo;mobile money&rdquo; into Google you will be served reams of articles about its runaway success in Africa. That&rsquo;s great PR for a continent used to bad press, but there is a problem. Mobile money has only really taken off in one country out of 55 on the continent.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Thanks to &lt;a href=\"http://www.safaricom.co.ke/?id=257\"&gt;M-Pesa in Kenya&lt;/a&gt;, the poster child of mobile money in Africa, there is a mistaken belief among some that it is the norm across Africa. The service allows people to use their phones like a bank account &ndash; depositing, withdrawing and transferring money with their handset. People can also pay utility bills and &ndash; less commonly &ndash; pay for goods in a limited number of shops.&nbsp; It currently has around 20 million subscribers &ndash; around half the population of the East African nation.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There are various reasons for this&ndash; a receptive regulatory environment, the dominant position of Safaricom, Kenya&rsquo;s biggest mobile operator and the owner of M-Pesa, as well as a clear demand for its &ldquo;send money&rdquo; function, due to high numbers of domestic migrants transferring funds back home.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But in other countries, the quest to replicate Kenya&rsquo;s success remains a difficult and uphill struggle. Attempts to roll out M-Pesa in countries like Tanzania and South Africa have faced a range of obstacles and have failed to emulate Kenya&rsquo;s success. While in countries like Nigeria, Uganda and Ghana, other mobile money projects are beginning to pay off, but there is still a long way to go.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It seems mobile money&nbsp;as a stand-alone offering does not excite most consumers who operate in economies where cash is still king. In fact research suggests that even if consumers know their network&nbsp;has a mobile money proposition, very few find any use for it.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But I believe that could change. In particular, I believe mobile-commerce (m-commerce) offers an opportunity to boost mobile money use and give economies across Africa a shot in the arm.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Push button&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Take an incident that happened to me in Ghana a few weeks ago. After dinner, I pulled out my debit card to pay only to be told by the waitress that it was a cash-only service. Yet, this was at one of the best restaurants in the country. For a continent supposed to be at the forefront of mobile money it was beyond belief.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Now, imagine if I were able to make that payment with my phone. With a simple tap I could have paid for the meal and been on my way. It is this kind of service &ndash; already offered in a limited way by M-Pesa - that I believe will allow mobile money to really take off. By allowing small and medium businesses to easily accept payments, it will create an environment where goods and services can be offered and bought via mobiles as easily as someone buying a book on Amazon or paying for a coffee in Starbucks with their debit card. In short, we need mobile payment systems that allow easy payments between companies and customers online and in the real world.&nbsp;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;M-commerce, powered by smart mobile payment systems, would radically change the way business is done.&nbsp; Most of sub-Saharan Africa remains a rurally driven agri-economy, with farmers and businessmen having to rely on their local market days to do business. Successful m-commerce would allow the farmers, businessmen and entrepreneurs of Africa to reach a wider range of possible buyers at a relatively low cost.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It would open the door for many online businesses to emerge, and the ease of purchase that will follow will spur consumption, create new jobs and help African companies to develop markets beyond their existing borders and the borders of the continent.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It would not only benefit rural dwellers but also their fellow countrymen who live in urban areas. From micro transactions such as purchasing a cinema ticket to those on a larger scale such as paying for white goods, m-commerce will enable people living in African cities to make the transactions and payments necessary for everyday living at the push of a button, without the need for cash.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In recent months a couple of forward thinking African start-ups have launched services looking to disrupt the way people currently pay for products. With banks and mobile networks spending huge sums to secure a piece of the mobile money action, these technology companies are already thinking of the future, building online shopping cart plug-ins that are accessible via mobiles and have the capability to process mobile payments. The key factor driving this innovation is payment -&nbsp; how to make it easier for people to pay for goods and services in markets, restaurants and cinemas as well as online without the need to resort to cash.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As with mobile banking, Kenya is leading the way. For example, &lt;a href=\"http://www.kopokopo.com/\"&gt;KopoKopo&lt;/a&gt; operates a platform that builds on top of M-Pesa to allow small and medium businesses move to accept mobile money payments at the shop till. Others like Kenya&rsquo;s &lt;a href=\"http://zegetech.com/home/mpayer/\"&gt;Mpayer&lt;/a&gt;, take the process one step further, allowing real world transactions as well as payments on the web and via web-enabled phones. It is not difficult to imagine the impact of such technology on small businesses, farmers, craftsmen, and food or beverage retailers.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But Kenya isn&rsquo;t the only place where mobile money is trying to hit critical mass. In Nigeria, established firms likes the UK&rsquo;s &lt;a href=\"http://www.monitise.com/\"&gt;Monitise&lt;/a&gt; are beginning to make inroads, while one of the recent success stories is a relatively new upstart, &lt;a href=\"https://www.mypaga.com/paga-web/start.paga\"&gt;Paga&lt;/a&gt;, backed by Silicon Valley money and well known investment funds.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The core business for Paga is its mobile money transfer service; however those behind it realise the importance of using their platform to facilitate payment for goods and services, and are signing up merchants across Nigeria to use the platform to receive payments. As with the Kenyan start-ups, the suspicion is that the company will pivot to focus more on consumer-to- merchant payments as opposed to peer-to-peer transfers.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Intel-backed Ghanaian technology firm Rancard Solutions and mobile operator Bharti Airtel also recently launched a mobile platform that allows their customers to buy and sell products via their phones. Furthermore, Airtel customers can also pay for purchases using their mobile wallet. Whether this will take off or not remains to be seen, but it is a welcome step in the right direction.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;I believe these kinds of innovations are just the beginning. In the coming years, we will see all kinds of new services being built and even more piggy-backing on them to create new businesses and value in Africa.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Then Africa will truly deserve the title of the home of mobile money.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook page&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:12:01Z","HeadlineLong":"Making mobile money pay in Africa","HeadlineShort":"Making mobile money pay ","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"A Matter of Life and Tech features a range of voices from people involved in Africas tech future. If the child saves it until he or she is 18 they would be able to automatically exchange it for a working bank account, identified by the barcode. The bank account would hold all the interest that accrued on the child&rsquo;s dollar since birth and the child would be the only one authorised to withdraw the money.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Importantly, the scheme would be funded by the fines levied against banks involved in unscrupulous practices, whose name would be imprinted on the each coin.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This plan has at least three benefits.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One, it would teach all children the benefits of saving and remove the barriers that the poorest everywhere face because they can&rsquo;t open a bank account.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Two, it would provide women who are often economically marginalised with their own source of income.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And three, it would serve as a powerful reminder to financial institutions that ethics count.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;You can listen to Catherine discuss her idea with angel investor Sherry Coutu and leading coffee entrepreneur Andrew Rugmore on the &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/programmes/p004kln9\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;BBC World Service programme The Forum&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;, where you can also download more 60-second ideas.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you have a 60-second idea or would like to comment on this story, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook page&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; our brains process rewards could be a factor (Copyright: Science Photo Library)","SynopsisMedium":"How our brains process rewards could be a factor (Copyright: Science Photo Library)","SynopsisShort":"What makes us extroverts and
