Thinkstock)","SynopsisMedium":"(Copyright: Thinkstock)","SynopsisShort":"What makes us extroverts and introverts?","TemplateUrl":"http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/$recipe/images/live/p0/1c/ws/p01cwszd.jpg","Title":"dv617058.jpg","CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p01cwszd","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p01cwszd","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p01cwszd","_id":"559a53e50b808738c4e2a178"}],"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Description":"&lt;p&gt;Tom is a Lecturer in Psychology and Cognitive Science for the Department\nof Psychology, University of Sheffield, UK. He is the co-author of the bestselling\npopular science book Mind Hacks and writes for the award-winning blog &lt;a href=\"http://mindhacks.com/\"&gt;Mind Hacks&lt;/a&gt; which reports on psychology and\nneuroscience. You can follow him on Twitter at &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/tomstafford\"&gt;@tomstafford&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;","Email":[],"Links":null,"Name":"Tom Stafford","PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:07Z","Entity":"author","Guid":"de5c2328-ffca-4de5-bb50-c350baf11d94","Id":"wwfuture/author/tom-stafford","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:07Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"tom-stafford"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:author:wwfuture/author/tom-stafford","_id":"559a524f0b808738c4e25fd4"}],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Will you spend Saturday night in a crowded bar, or curled up with a good book? Is your ideal holiday adventure sports with a large group of mates and, or anywhere more sedate destination with a few good friends? Maybe your answers to these questions are clear &ndash; you'd love one option and hate another &ndash; or maybe you find yourself somewhere between the two extremes. Whatever your answers, the origin of your feelings may lie in how your brain responds to rewards.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;We all exist somewhere on the spectrum between extroverts and introverts, and different circumstances can make us feel more one way or the other. Extraverts, a term popularised by psychologist Carl Jung at the beginning of the 20th Century, seem to dominate our world, either because they really are more common, or because they just make most of the noise. (The original spelling of &ldquo;extravert&rdquo; is now rarely used generally, but is still used in psychology.) This is so much the case that some have even written guides on &lt;a href=\"http://www.theatlantic.com/magazine/archive/2003/03/caring-for-your-introvert/302696/\"&gt;how to care for introverts&lt;/a&gt;, and nurture their special talents.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;A fundamental question remains &ndash; what makes an extrovert? Why are we all different in this respect, and what do extraverts have in common that makes them like they are? Now, with brain scans that can record activity from deep within the brain, and with genetic profiling that reveals the code behind the constructions of the chemical signalling system used by the brain, we can put some answers to these decades-old questions.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In the 1960s, psychologist Hans Eysenck made the &lt;a href=\"http://books.google.co.uk/books/about/The_Biological_Basis_of_Personality.html?id=unee8oL0OgwC&amp;redir_esc=y\"&gt;influential proposal&lt;/a&gt; that extroverts were defined by having a chronically lower level of arousal. Arousal, in the physiological sense, is the extent to which our bodies and minds are alert and ready to respond to stimulation. This varies for us all throughout the day (for example, as I move from asleep to awake, usually via few cups of coffee) and in different circumstances (for example, cycling through the rush-hour keeps you on your toes, heightening arousal, whereas a particularly warm lecture theatre tends to lower your arousal). Eysenck's theory was that extroverts have just a slightly lower basic rate of arousal. The effect is that they need to work a little harder to get themselves up to the level others find normal and pleasant without doing anything. Hence the need for company, seeking out novel experiences and risks. Conversely, highly introverted individuals find themselves overstimulated by things others might find merely pleasantly exciting or engaging. Hence they seek out quiet conversations about important topics, solitary pursuits and predictable environments.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Betting brains&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;More recently, this theory has been refined, linking extroversion to the function of &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/11301519\"&gt;dopamine&lt;/a&gt;, a chemical that plays an intimate role in the brain circuits which control reward, learning and responses to novelty. Could extroverts differ in how active their dopamine systems are? This would provide a neat explanation for the kinds of behaviours extroverts display, while connecting it to an aspect of brain function that we know quite a lot about for other reasons.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Researchers lead by Michael Cohen, now of the University of Amsterdam, were able to test these ideas in &lt;a href=\"http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0926641005002880\"&gt;a paper published in 2005&lt;/a&gt;. They asked participants to perform a gambling task while in the brain scanner. Before they went in the scanner each participant filled out a personality profile and contributed a mouth swab for genetic analysis. Analysis of the imaging data showed how the brain activity differed between extroverted volunteers and introverted ones. When the gambles they took paid off, the more extroverted group showed a stronger response in two crucial brain regions: the amygdala and the nucleus accumbens. The amygdala is known for processing emotional stimuli, and the nucleus accumbens is a key part of the brain's reward circuitry and part of the dopamine system. The results confirm the theory &ndash; extroverts process surprising rewards differently.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;When Cohen's group looked at the genetic profiles of the participants, they found another difference in reward-related brain activity. Those volunteers who had a gene known to increase the responsiveness of the dopamine system also showed increased activity when they won a gamble.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So here we see part of the puzzle of why we're all different in this way. Extrovert's brains respond more strongly when gambles pay off. Obviously they are going to enjoy adventure sports more, or social adventures like meeting new people more. Part of this difference is genetic, resulting from the way our genes shape and develop our brains. Other results confirm that dopamine function is key to this &ndash; so, for example, genes that control dopamine function &lt;a href=\"http://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s11055-007-0058-8#page-1\"&gt;predict personality differences&lt;/a&gt; in how much people enjoy the unfamiliar and actively seek out novelty. Other results show how &lt;a href=\"http://www.frontiersin.org/Human_Neuroscience/10.3389/fnhum.2013.00288/abstract\"&gt;extroverts learn differently&lt;/a&gt;, in keeping with a heighted sensitivity to rewards due to their reactive dopamine systems.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Our preferences are shaped by the way our brains respond to the world. Maybe this little bit of biological psychology can help us all, whether introverts or extroverts, by allowing us to appreciate how and why others might like different things from us.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this video or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook page&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":null,"CollectionType":"column","Description":"Neuroscience and the psychology of the everyday. Psychologist Tom Stafford explores the surprisingly complex theories behind experiences we all take for granted.","Name":"Neurohacks","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Neurohacks","CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:56.413888Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fcdb0306-a2e2-447c-ae2c-cf60ee943f26","Id":"wwfuture/column/neurohacks","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:56.413888Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"column/neurohacks"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:56.413888Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fcdb0306-a2e2-447c-ae2c-cf60ee943f26","Id":"wwfuture/column/neurohacks","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:56.413888Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"column/neurohacks"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/column/neurohacks","_id":"559a52610b808738c4e262a9"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Health","Name":"Health","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Health"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:44:06.503434Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"00a9a33a-e393-4d09-8029-7e9d5bd1d8cb","Id":"wwfuture/section/health","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:52:37.074442Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/health"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/health","_id":"559a52620b808738c4e262db"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Science &amp; Environment","Name":"Science &amp; Environment","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Science &amp; Environment"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:51:45.984509Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"1aa4ccec-87b5-44b8-b5af-c553debaf6f6","Id":"wwfuture/section/science-environment","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:51:20.083392Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/science-environment"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/science-environment","_id":"559a52620b808738c4e262dc"}],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-07-17T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"What makes us extroverts and introverts?","HeadlineShort":"What makes someone an extrovert?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Why do some people prefer adventure and the company of others, while others favour being alone? Its all to do with how your brain processes rewards. ","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":[],"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;It&rsquo;s definitely not a bird. Nor is it a plane. The garish orange piece of plastic, small enough to hold in the palm of a hand, could pass for a missing limb of a toy tyrannosaurus. It may not look all that impressive, but it&rsquo;s notable for two reasons. One is that the monster arm has emerged from a 3D printer. The other is that it is, in fact, the first ever object made from thought.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This milestone was reached with little fanfare last month at the &lt;a href=\"http://stgomakerspace.com\"&gt;&lt;span&gt;Santiago MakerSpace&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, a technology and design studio in the Chilean capital. The toy limb&rsquo;s shape was determined according to the wishes of its designer, as gleaned from a headset picking up his brainwaves. The man in question was George Laskowsky, Chief Technical Officer of &lt;a href=\"http://www.thinkerthing.com/\"&gt;Thinker Thing&lt;/a&gt;, the Chilean start-up developing the mind-controlled 3D printing system.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Engineers and designers have been using 3D printers for more than two decades. More recently, prices have tumbled and desk-top devices are increasingly being pitched at consumers. The touted possibilities appear to be endless &ndash; from bones to buildings to burritos &ndash; making some observers predict revolutionary consequences like the eventual demise of the factory. Because 3D printers build objects layer by layer from materials such as plastic or metal dust, a key advantage is the comparative freedom they give designers. Yet the design software is not easy to master, especially if you are four-years-old and haven&rsquo;t yet learnt to hold a pencil properly.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;What is the point of these printers if my son cannot design his own toy?&rdquo; says Bryan Salt, CEO of Thinker Thing. &ldquo;I realised that while there were a lot of people talking about the hardware of the printer no-one really seemed to be talking about how to actually use it.&rdquo; In theory 3D printers could help unleash our inner creativity, freeing us from the constraints of traditional production methods. However, in practice those unwilling or unable to plough through the software instruction manual could be left downloading ready- made models designed by others.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;That&rsquo;s where Emotional Evolutionary Design (EED), the software that allows Thinker Thing to interpret its users&rsquo; thoughts, comes in. Its current role is to power the Monster Dreamer Project, which will allow users to design their own fantastical creatures using the power of thought. Chilean children will get the first opportunity to try it out during tour of schools in the country at the end of this month.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;When those children sit in front of a computer running Monster Dreamer, they will be presented with a series of different body shapes in bubbles. These will mutate randomly, with built-in rules preventing them becoming too abstract. The children&rsquo;s reactions to the changes will be picked up by an &lt;a href=\"http://www.emotiv.com/\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Emotiv EPOC headset&lt;/a&gt;, a $300 electroencephalography (EEG) device designed to pick up the electrical signals from brain cell interactions using fourteen sensors on the scalp. As different brain states such as excitement or boredom generate specific patterns of brain activity, the computer can identify the shapes associated with positive emotional responses. The favoured shapes will grow bigger on the screen, while the others shrink. The biggest shapes are combined to generate a body part, and the process is repeated for different body parts until the monster is complete. The final result should be a unique 3D model that is ready for printing as a solid object.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Second nature&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Design steered by emotional responses is based on the notion that most people are better at critiquing a design than they are at thinking of new ideas from scratch, especially if they have no training. &ldquo;One of the biggest bottlenecks right now with 3D printing is content,&rdquo; says Professor Hod Lipson, director of the &lt;a href=\"http://creativemachines.cornell.edu/\"&gt;Creative Machines Lab&lt;/a&gt; at Cornell University, in Ithaca, New York State. &ldquo;We have iPods with no music. We have machines that can make almost anything but we do not have a lot of things to make with them.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Lipson&rsquo;s lab is also working on evolving 3D models with the mind. &lt;a href=\"http://endlessforms.com/\"&gt;EndlessForms&lt;/a&gt;, created by two of Lipson&rsquo;s students, is a website that mimics nature&rsquo;s way of creating new designs in small steps. At the start of the process users are presented with 15 three-dimensional shapes. Clicking on any two will combine them and produce fifteen new shapes based on those choices. If you wanted to make a cat, you might click on one shape with the semblance of a muzzle and another with two pointed, ear-like triangles on top. The computer would then offer up a series of new shapes that more closely resemble the cat you have in mind, and so on until the model reaches the desired shape.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;To reduce the time spent clicking, the researchers came to the same conclusion as the Thinker Thing team &ndash; feeding users&rsquo; thoughts back into the computer directly could make the process quicker. So, last year, the team used Emotiv EPOC headsets to read users&rsquo; brain signals and therefore determine their reactions. But then they ran into a problem. &ldquo;At some point we were thinking it was only measuring the level of sweat because we were actually trying so hard to feel happy or sad about something,&rdquo; says Lipson. No matter how many times they tried, the scientists could not find a reliable signal to use from the headset.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The problem with cheap consumer headsets is that the signals they pick up are already weak. The skull dampens the small electrical impulses from the brain&rsquo;s neurons and electrical signals from nearby facial muscles can overpower them. Some sceptics argue that consumer EEG devices are not really measuring thoughts at all.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Others argue that for applications that only require basic feedback such as yes or no, the readings they generate can be accurate enough. &ldquo;If they are simple positive or negative emotions, it can be 100%,&rdquo; says Dr Olga Sourina, head of the &lt;a href=\"http://www.ntu.edu.sg/home/eosourina/CHCILab/index.html\"&gt;Cognitive Human Computer Interaction Lab&lt;/a&gt; at the Nanyang Technological University, Singapore. &ldquo;When you need to differ between more emotions like anger or fear, they can be less accurate.&rdquo; Sourina has spent nine years working on improving the ability of computers to recognise emotions from EEG.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;With some of the limitations of consumer EEG technology in mind, Lipson and colleagues decided to monitor thoughts via the eyeballs. Eye tracking can identify the shapes that get the most attention, and this could be used to shape design processes. The snag is that without an EEG headset it is not possible to tell whether someone is looking at a shape because they find it strange or beautiful. Even so, the team&rsquo;s research so far suggests that at the end of the process participants still feel they have managed to reach the desired design. There&rsquo;s some way to go, but in the future a combination of brain scanning and eye tracking may be preferred to the trusty old mouse when it comes to 3D object design.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Dream maker&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;If and when that day comes, it still won&rsquo;t be a case of closing your eyes, imagining a unicorn and hearing the printer take off. But recent work by two teams of neuroscientists suggests the idea of translating whole images from the mind to the design screen may not be as improbable as it sounds. &ldquo;In principle, it is not farfetched at all,&rdquo; says Professor Jack Gallant, from the University of California, Berkeley. &ldquo;We have already published many papers where we reconstruct photographs or movies from what people have seen.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In 2011, Gallant&rsquo;s team used a magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) scanner to show how computers &lt;a href=\"http://ac.els-cdn.com/S0960982211009377/1-s2.0-S0960982211009377-main.pdf?_tid=31af21f8-d357-11e2-b72e-00000aacb35d&amp;acdnat=1371038402_ec24ef5f425036a951f7730e0190d837\"&gt;can be trained to read the minds&lt;/a&gt; of those watching moving images. They built up a database of the activity in a key visual centre of the brain as three fellow researchers watched a compilation of Hollywood film trailer clips. Next the subjects watched a new set of clips. Based on the brain activity this generated, and the database created from the first phase of the experiment, the computer was asked to select segments of 5,000 hours of randomly selected YouTube clips that best matched the second sequence of clips. &lt;a href=\"http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=FR0qJ17Rsvc\"&gt;The results&lt;/a&gt;, although blurry, were recognisable as copies of the originals, and demonstrated for the first time the ability to decode moving images from the brain.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;A related advance came in April this year when Japanese scientists led by Yukiyasu Kamitani, of the ATR Computational Neuroscience Laboratories in Kyoto, revealed they had made significant steps towards &lt;a href=\"http://neurosurgery.washington.edu/Lectures/science.1234330.full.pdf\"&gt;automated dream decoding&lt;/a&gt;. Three people had their brains scanned in a MRI machine while they slept. They were awoken when EEG signals indicated they had reached an early phase of sleep associated with dreaming. The researchers then asked them to describe their dreams, with the process being repeated until more than 200 reports had been collected for each person.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Kamitani&rsquo;s group then chose 20 categories of objects and scenes based on the words that occurred most frequently in the descriptions. They selected photos representing each category, and scanned their volunteers&rsquo; brains while they looked at them. Comparing the scans taken while participants were awake with those while they were dreaming allowed the accurate prediction of dream content 60-70% of the time, depending on the individuals, the brain areas involved and different objects and scenes involved. It may not yet be a fully formed &ldquo;dream decoder&rdquo;, but it does show that direct decoding mental images is a possibility.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;MRI scanners may be better at reading the brain than cheap EEG headsets, but that does not make them a practical or affordable solution for 3D printing. &ldquo;As a giant three million dollar magnet, it is not something you would just wear around,&rdquo; says Gallant.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Even if these issues can be overcome, there are other obstacles. Seeing an object and imagining one may not produce the same brain signals. On top of this individuals vary widely in their abilities to dream up designs from scratch, and in the level of detail they can imagine. &ldquo;I have been doing 3D modelling since it began back in the 80s,&rdquo; said Salt. &ldquo;And the process is that you build something and then you move it about. You do not sit down and think, I have something absolutely finite in my head and that is what I am going to build.&rdquo;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;These challenges suggest the idea of 3D printing guided by fully formed mental images of users is, if not entirely farfetched, a long way from becoming reality. Combining sensors that can pick up human emotions with design software that can interpret and respond to them looks like the nearest we&rsquo;re going to get to creating 3D objects from thought in the near future.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So Thinker Thing&rsquo;s twig-like orange monster arm, as unsophisticated as it may appear at first glance, may one day be celebrated as marking the start of a new and exciting way of moulding the things around us. &ldquo;It is really something magical to be there, sat without moving a limb, and watching the designs evolve into something that you were thinking about,&rdquo; says Laskowsky.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this story or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;span&gt;Facebook&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;span&gt;Twitter&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-06-14T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"3D printing powered by thought","HeadlineShort":"3D printing your thoughts","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Imagine if you could print objects just by thinking about them. Camila Ruz visits one company to see whether this is far-fetched dream or a real possibility. ","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"slideshow","SummaryLong":"Imagine if you could print objects just by thinking about them. Camila Ruz visits one company to see whether this is far-fetched dream or a real possibility. ","SummaryShort":"A far-fetched dream or a real possibility?","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:00Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"1b2ad8b6-dcfc-41b5-9877-5f6e60c02e8d","Id":"wwfuture/story/20130613-3d-printing-your-thoughts","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:00Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20130613-3d-printing-your-thoughts"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20130613-3d-printing-your-thoughts","_id":"559a56130b808738c4e2f3a3"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":null,"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Sometimes the results of science experiments are just too weird to believe. In 2005 Jennifer Pluznick hypothesised that a gene known to play a role in a common form of kidney disease did so by acting as a master switch for other genes. Lab tests supported her theory; however, when she looked at which genes it acted on, she did a double take. Among them were several that encode scent receptors, the chemical sensors that allow us to identify smells.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;At first Pluznick didn't believe her eyes. As far as we know, kidneys filter waste from our blood and get rid of it in urine. They are not sensory organs. \"I wasn't sure if we could believe the data,\" she says. \"It seemed really spurious.\" Thinking there had to be a mistake, Pluznick, then a researcher at Yale School of Medicine, in New Haven, Connecticut, carried out another experiment. She soaked a slice of kidney in a fluid that would make scent-system proteins glow fluorescently under a microscope. When she peered down the eyepiece, it was lit up like a Christmas tree.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Pluznick has spent the last eight years trying to understand why.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The sense of smell is one of our most powerful connections to the physical world. Our noses contain hundreds of different scent receptors that allow us to distinguish between odours. When you smell a rose or a pot of beef stew, the brain is responding to scent molecules that have wafted into your nose and locked on to these receptors. Only certain molecules fit specific receptors, and when they slot together, like a key in a lock, this triggers changes in cells. In the case of scent receptors, specialised neurons send messages to the brain so we know what we have sniffed. The discovery, in 1991, of around 1,000 genes involved in generating scent receptors was rewarded with the &lt;a href=\"http://www.nobelprize.org/nobel_prizes/medicine/laureates/2004/\"&gt;Nobel prize in Medicine&lt;/a&gt; over a decade later.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In the last ten years, however, reports have trickled in from bemused biologists that these receptors, as well as similar ones usually found on taste buds, crop up all over our bodies. In 2003, bitter taste receptors were found in sperm. The same year Pluznick came across scent receptors in the kidney, biologists at the University of California, San Diego identified sour receptors in the spine. A smattering of papers over the following few years reported sweet taste receptors in the bladder and the gut, bitter taste receptors in the sinuses, airways, pancreas and brain, and scent receptors in muscle tissue.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;What are they doing there? As these findings became public, researchers poured over genomic data and reported that low levels of these receptors occurred in almost every tissue in the body. One possible explanation was that they are evolutionary leftovers whose previous functions were lost in time. However, Pluznick and others have unearthed evidence that, in some tissues at least, they are anything but passive. Their findings suggest that our bodies are &ldquo;smelling&rdquo; and &ldquo;tasting&rdquo; things deep inside of us, and that these abilities are crucial to our health.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;System sensor&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The kidney is made up of miles of minuscule tubes, twisted into an exquisite lacework. Threaded through these is a system of blood vessels, and in places where these and the kidney's tubes are especially intertwined, water, nutrients and other molecules seep through the walls and pass between the two. What the blood doesn't need is passed off to the urine forming in the tubes, and what it does have a use for &ndash; keeping blood pressure stable, for instance &ndash; goes back. If this process stops working the body will soon shut down, poisoned by its own exhaust fumes.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Peering more closely down the microscope at that slice of kidney, Pluznick saw the fluorescent glow was emanating from the macula densa, a group of cells that play a central role in this chemical back-and-forth, sampling the forming urine as it goes by and sending out alerts to adjust the blood filtration rate. &ldquo;The macula densa is the only cell type in the kidney that you would think of as being a chemical sensor,&rdquo; says Pluznick. &ldquo;It made a hell of a lot of sense, and it made me think it was real.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The next step was to figure out what would happen if these receptors were absent in this region of the kidney, using engineered mice that lacked one of scent receptors Pluznick identified in the kidney, called Olfr78. Tests revealed these mice had kidney problems involving their blood filtration rate and the production of renin, a hormone which stimulates the constriction of blood vessels to increase blood pressure. Both of these are controlled by the macula densa.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Pluznick now began to devote herself full-time to the puzzle. Identifying which chemical \"keys\" &ndash; known as ligands &ndash; were binding to the Olfr78 receptor \"locks\" would help her understand the bigger picture of what the receptor was responding to. In doing so, her work took her from one weird and wonderful discovery in biology to another: &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20120412-the-beasts-inside-you\"&gt;the microbiome&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Our bodies contain trillions of bacteria, fungi, and other microorganisms. Those in the human gut fulfil various important functions, such as breaking down certain foods into energy and useful nutrients, suppressing harmful microbes, preventing allergies and assisting the immune system in a number of other ways. The evidence that the balance of different microbes we have inside us is important to our health has been growing rapidly in recent years. People with irritable bowel syndrome, obesity, Alzheimer's disease, Parkinson's and depression have been shown to have differences in gut bacteria when compared to healthy people.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As a &lt;a href=\"http://www.pnas.org/content/early/2013/02/05/1215927110\"&gt;study published by Pluznick last March&lt;/a&gt; showed, Olfr78's ligands are short-chain fatty acids, molecules produced when gut bacteria digest components of plant-based foods. Other scientists have documented their roles in human health, such as stimulating immune-cell production, reducing heart-disease risk, stabilising blood glucose levels and protecting the colon lining.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Pluznick acknowledges the picture is a complex and incomplete one, further work indicates another receptor that also binds to short-chain fatty acids has a greater and opposite effect. However, she believes the work shows the scent receptors she accidentally discovered are a previously unknown means for bacteria to tell the kidney to make blood pressure changes that allow them to best carry out their health-related functions.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Bitter truth&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As Pluznick was unravelling this mystery, other researchers were investigating taste and scent receptors elsewhere in the body. Yehuda Ben-Shahar, now a professor at Washington University in St. Louis, found cells in the human airway equipped with bitter receptors. These cells are covered in microscopic hair-like protrusions called cilia which flap when dangerous chemicals are breathed in, helping to flush them out of the body. Bitter receptors in the tongue are thought to have evolved to help us identify poisons, and Ben-Shahar wondered whether those in the airway might be protecting us in a similar way.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;He and his collaborators found that when receptors in these cells were exposed to certain noxious molecules, it triggered a cascade of events that culminated in the flapping of the cilia. This made Ben-Shahar think that what we know as bitter receptors might better be called danger receptors. &ldquo;We call them bitter but that's only because when we taste with them that's what we get,\" he says. \"Probably what's uniting them is things that we try not to take in.\"&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In fact, work by Noam Cohen, an ear, nose and throat doctor at University of Pennsylvania Medical School, suggests an intriguing role for bitter receptors recently discovered in the sinuses. He found one particular type can intercept the chemical signals that bacteria send to each other when they are coming together to form biofilms, a manoeuvre that greatly strengthens their defences against immune-system attacks.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;When the bitter receptors in the sinuses pick up these signals, they set in motion an attack against the bacteria, causing cells to release toxic gas and cilia to flap. If people have a genetic variant that produces a different form of this bitter receptor, however, they are deaf to this back-channel bacterial chatter, and are more prone to severe sinus infections.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Patients who regularly get serious sinus infections often opt for surgery. However Cohen, who, along with Ben-Shahar, presented some of his findings at April's Association for Chemoreception Sciences meeting in Huntington Beach, California, speculates this may not help those with bitter receptors that can't pick up these signals. Since the bitter receptors in an individual's sinuses are the same as those on their tongue, Cohen suggests a test that tells clinicians which version of the receptor patients have on their tongues could help guide treatment.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Different tastes&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Pluznick's research has revealed the existence of Olfr 78 receptors beyond the kidney, in blood vessels in the skin, heart and muscle. Scent receptors may be even more widely distributed, Firestein thinks, in blood vessels right across in the body. There's no better place for a chemical sensor, he points out, as the blood is the highway by which substances move around the body. This could explain why the receptors show up in so many places, since tissues are shot through with blood vessels bringing in nutrients and ferrying away waste.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;What's emerging is a picture of these receptors as a kind of general-purpose chemical sensor. Consider a doorbell: the button next to your front door is hooked up to a little machine that can be programmed to play almost any kind of tune, a clock chime, a fire alarm, or \"Wild Thing\" if so desired. The button doesn't care what is being played &ndash; it just relays to the machine the message that a finger is pushing on it. These receptors are like that button, and the finger is anything with the right chemical structure to bind to it. The output might be the perception of a scent, the alerting of the immune system or the beating of cilia. &ldquo;If you say olfactory receptors in the kidney, it sounds kind of nuts,\" says Pluznick. \"But if you say that chemical sensors are in the kidney, I think it makes a heck of a lot of sense.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;We just happened to come across the receptors in the nose and the mouth first. It's intriguing to think this role may be pre-dated by their chemical-sensing capacities in other organs. Some see it as unlikely given that they are present in much higher numbers in the nose and mouth, but others disagree. &ldquo;We call these taste receptors, but maybe they aren't taste receptors,\" says Ben-Shahar. \"Maybe they were doing a lot of things before. Recycling is a very strong theme in evolution.&rdquo; Cohen puts it more strongly. \"I'm willing to bet that these bitter receptors were on the airway before they migrated to the tongue, that this is where they really belong.\"&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Pluznick is now a professor at Johns Hopkins School of Medicine, in Baltimore, Maryland. It has been a long time since she studied kidney disease. Her lab is focused on understanding how these receptors work, and how they contribute to kidney function. Pluznick acknowledges there is still a lot to discover about the broader functions of what we still currently call scent and taste receptors, but she and others have made a start. She chuckles as she remembers how she complained to her academic supervisor about the \"bad\" data from her kidney gene experiments.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;\"He kind of looked at me for a second, and he was like 'Scent receptors in the kidney. That would be cool though, right?' At that point we both still thought it was one of those crazy, stupid ideas you laugh about later.\" Crazy perhaps, but very far from stupid.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;CORRRECTION (12/07)&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;:&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;em&gt; We stated that the engineered mice used in experiements had come from Stuart Firestein's lab, but this was not the case. This has been corrected.&nbsp; &lt;br /&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-07-10T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"The startling sense of smell found all over your body","HeadlineShort":"How our organs sniff out smells","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Bemused biologists have found the machinery that helps us identify smells and tastes crops up everywhere  from our kidneys to sperm. After many years they think they might know why, as Veronique Greenwood reports.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"Bemused biologists have found our machinery for identifying odours and tastes also exists in kidneys, airways and sperm. They think they finally might know why.","SummaryShort":"Bizarre finding shows vital sensor system","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:07Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"24df1253-10b1-4a97-881f-cc85fdd72442","Id":"wwfuture/story/20130710-how-our-organs-sniff-out-smells","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:07Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20130710-how-our-organs-sniff-out-smells"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20130710-how-our-organs-sniff-out-smells","_id":"559a56160b808738c4e2f3f9"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"ib2video","AssetVideoIb2":[],"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":null,"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;There&rsquo;s now a great deal of effort and money devoted to understanding the extraordinary complexity of the human brain. But it&rsquo;s also important to go back to basics, says &lt;a href=\"http://wi.mit.edu/people/faculty/lindquist\"&gt;Susan Lindquist&lt;/a&gt;, molecular biologist at Massachusetts Institute of Technology. Her idea is to use the power of evolution to unlock secrets that could keep our minds healthy as we age.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As we reduce the rate of people dying from heart disease and cancer, we face another threat, and that&rsquo;s neurodegenerative diseases, like Alzheimer&rsquo;s and Parkinson&rsquo;s disease, that are caused by fundamental defects in protein folding. Studies have been dominated by the need to unlock the complexity of the brain, but you can flip this around and look at what are the fundamental basics of biology that might also feature in much simpler organisms.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;We evolved from simpler individual cells that still inhabit the Earth, and they still face the same complex problem &ndash; how do they take their DNA code and create and fold it up into a complete, working protein. So we could test the simpler things first using these simple cells, and fuel our knowledge not just for possible drugs but also understand the biology in such a way as to help improve our lifestyle.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The challenge is how to go from these simple cells to making it work in a human being. That&rsquo;s going to take a lot of interaction between many different groups &ndash; like biologists, chemists, geneticists &ndash; not to mention new imaging methods for viewing the brain. But the way technologies are converging makes Lindquist excited and optimistic; she says we might now be in a position to begin to find answers that will help tackle these diseases.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this video or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-05-15T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Simpler solutions for unlocking secrets of ageing minds","HeadlineShort":"Unlocking secrets of ageing minds","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Could tiny organisms help us understand the extraordinary complexity of the human brain, and how to keep our brains healthy as we get older? One person thinks so.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"video","SummaryLong":"Could tiny organisms help us understand the extraordinary complexity of the human brain, and how to keep our brains healthy as we get older?","SummaryShort":"Simpler way to understand ailing brains","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:11:45Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"9d262bbb-5e86-4c75-ae7b-d62fac4152ec","Id":"wwfuture/story/20130514-unlocking-secrets-of-ageing-minds","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:11:45Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20130514-unlocking-secrets-of-ageing-minds"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20130514-unlocking-secrets-of-ageing-minds","_id":"559a56110b808738c4e2f34b"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;It's a tightening at the back of the throat, or a tingling around your scalp, a chill that comes over you when you pay close attention to something, such as a person whispering instructions. It's called the autonomous sensory meridian response, and until 2010 it didn't exist.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;I first heard about the autonomous sensory meridian response (ASMR) from British journalist &lt;a href=\"http://www.independent.co.uk/life-style/gadgets-and-tech/features/maria-spends-20-minutes-folding-towels-why-millions-are-mesmerised-by-asmr-videos-7956866.html\"&gt;Rhodri Marsden&lt;/a&gt;. He had become mesmerised by intentionally boring videos he found on YouTube, things like people explaining how to &lt;a href=\"http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=CHiKxytbCWk\"&gt;fold towels&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href=\"http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=1AK-eOx4RWc\"&gt;running hair dryers&lt;/a&gt; or role-playing &lt;a href=\"http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Xxh3tzbhwZE\"&gt;interactions with dentists&lt;/a&gt;. Millions of people were watching the videos, reportedly for the pleasurable sensations they generated.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Rhodri asked my opinion as a psychologist. Could this be a real thing? \"Sure,\" I said. If people say they feel it, it has to be real &ndash; in some form or another. The question is what kind of real is it? Are all these people experiencing the same thing? Is it learnt, or something we are born with? How common is it? Those are the kind of questions we'd ask as psychologists. But perhaps the most interesting thing about the ASMR is what happened to it before psychologists put their minds to it.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Presumably the feeling has existed for all of human history. Each person discovered the experience, treasured it or ignored it, and kept the feeling to themselves. That there wasn't a name for it until 2010 suggests that most people who had this feeling hadn't talked about it. It's amazing that it got this far without getting a name. In scientific terms, it didn't exist.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But then, of course, along came the 21st Century and, like they say, even if you're one in a million there's thousands of you on the internet. Now there's websites, &lt;a href=\"http://www.reddit.com/r/asmr/\"&gt;discussion forums&lt;/a&gt;, even a &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Autonomous_sensory_meridian_response\"&gt;Wikipedia page&lt;/a&gt;. And a name. In fact, many names &ndash; &ldquo;Attention Induced Euphoria&rdquo;, &ldquo;braingasm&rdquo;, or &ldquo;the unnamed feeling&rdquo; are all competing labels that haven't caught on in the same way as ASMR.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Myth becomes fact &lt;br /&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This points to something curious about the way we create knowledge, illustrated by a wonderful story about the &lt;a href=\"http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/1995Metic..30R.540M\"&gt;scientific history of meteorites&lt;/a&gt;. Rocks falling from the sky were considered myths in Europe for centuries, even though stories of their fiery trails across the sky, and actual rocks, were widely, if irregularly reported. The problem was that the kind of people who saw meteorites and subsequently collected them tended to be the kind of people who worked outdoors &ndash; that is, farmers and other country folk. You can imagine the scholarly minds of the Renaissance didn't weigh too heavily on their testimonies. Then in 1794 a meteorite shower fell on the town of Siena in Italy. Not only was Siena a town, it was a town with a university. The testimony of the townsfolk, including well-to-do church ministers and tourists, was impossible to deny and the reports written up in scholarly publications. Siena played a crucial part in the process of myth becoming fact.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Where early science required authorities and written evidence to turn myth into fact, ASRM shows that something more democratic can achieve the same result. Discussion among ordinary people on the internet provided validation that the unnamed feeling was a shared one. Suddenly many individuals who might have thought of themselves as unusual were able to recognise that they were a single group, with a common experience.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There is a blind spot in psychology for individual differences. ASMR has some similarities with synaesthesia (the merging of the senses where colours can have tastes, for example, or sounds produce visual effects). Both are extremes of normal sensation, which exist for some individuals but not others. For many years synaesthesia was a scientific backwater, a condition viewed as unproductive to research, perhaps just the product of people's imagination rather than a real sensory phenomenon. This changed when techniques were developed that precisely measured the effects of synaesthesia, demonstrating that it was far more than people&rsquo;s imagination. Now it has its own research community, with &lt;a href=\"http://www.synesthesia.info/\"&gt;conferences&lt;/a&gt; and papers in &lt;a href=\"http://www.ingentaconnect.com/content/imp/jcs/2001/00000008/00000012/1244\"&gt;scientific journals&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Perhaps ASMR will go the same way. Some people are certainly pushing for &lt;a href=\"http://www.asmr-research.org/\"&gt;research into it&lt;/a&gt;. As far as I know there are no systematic scientific studies on ASMR. Since I was quoted in that &lt;a href=\"http://www.independent.co.uk/life-style/gadgets-and-tech/features/maria-spends-20-minutes-folding-towels-why-millions-are-mesmerised-by-asmr-videos-7956866.html\"&gt;newspaper article&lt;/a&gt;, I've been contacted regularly by people interested in the condition and wanting to know about research into it. When people hear that their unnamed feeling has a name they are drawn to find out more, they want to know the reality of the feeling, and to connect with others who have it. Something common to all of us wants to validate our inner experience by having it recognised by other people, and in particular by the authority of science. I can't help &ndash; almost all I know about ASMR is in this column you are reading now. For now all we have is a name, but that's progress.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;For more discussion about ASMR, visit the &lt;a href=\"http://www.reddit.com/r/asmr/\"&gt;Reddit discussion page&lt;/a&gt;. If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-05-07T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"The people who find pleasure in being bored","HeadlineShort":"Does boredom give you pleasure?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Millions of people say they are thrilled by watching long, dull videos of folding towels or running hair dryers. Why? It could be a curious condition that didnt officially exist until 2010.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"Millions of people say they love watching long, dull videos of folding towels or hair dryers. Why? It could be a curious condition that didnt exist until 2010.","SummaryShort":"Curious condition found in millions of people","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:01Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"41b06c3b-99a4-41d5-ab12-7d5ee475caac","Id":"wwfuture/story/20130507-does-boredom-give-you-pleasure","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:01Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20130507-does-boredom-give-you-pleasure"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20130507-does-boredom-give-you-pleasure","_id":"559a56100b808738c4e2f337"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Why do we like music? Like most good questions, this one works on many levels. We have answers on some levels, but not all.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;We like music because it makes us feel good. Why does it make us feel good? In 2001, neuroscientists Anne Blood and Robert Zatorre at McGill University in Montreal &lt;a href=\"http://www.pnas.org/content/98/20/11818.long\"&gt;provided an answer&lt;/a&gt;. Using magnetic resonance imaging they showed that people listening to pleasurable music had activated brain regions called the limbic and paralimbic areas, which are connected to euphoric reward responses, like those we experience from sex, good food and addictive drugs. Those rewards come from a gush of a neurotransmitter called dopamine. As DJ Lee Haslam told us, music is the drug.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But why? It&rsquo;s easy enough to understand why sex and food are rewarded with a dopamine rush: this makes us want more, and so contributes to our survival and propagation. (Some drugs subvert that survival instinct by stimulating dopamine release on false pretences.) But why would a sequence of sounds with no obvious survival value do the same thing?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The truth is no one knows. However, we now have many clues to why music provokes intense emotions. The current favourite theory among scientists who study the cognition of music &ndash; how we process it mentally &ndash; dates back to 1956, when the philosopher and composer &lt;a href=\"http://www.press.uchicago.edu/ucp/books/book/chicago/E/bo3643659.html\"&gt;Leonard Meyer suggested&lt;/a&gt; that emotion in music is all about what we expect, and whether or not we get it. Meyer drew on earlier psychological theories of emotion, which proposed that it arises when we&rsquo;re unable to satisfy some desire. That, as you might imagine, creates frustration or anger &ndash; but if we then find what we&rsquo;re looking for, be it love or a cigarette, the payoff is all the sweeter.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This, Meyer argued, is what music does too. It sets up sonic patterns and regularities that tempt us to make unconscious predictions about what&rsquo;s coming next. If we&rsquo;re right, the brain gives itself a little reward &ndash; as we&rsquo;d now see it, a surge of dopamine. The constant dance between expectation and outcome thus enlivens the brain with a pleasurable play of emotions.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Why should we care, though, whether our musical expectations are right or not? It&rsquo;s not as if our life depended on them. Ah, says musicologist &lt;a href=\"http://www.doc.gold.ac.uk/%7Emas03dm/papers/huron06-review.pdf\"&gt;David Huron&lt;/a&gt; of Ohio State University, but perhaps once it did. Making predictions about our environment &ndash; interpreting what we see and hear, say, on the basis of only partial information &ndash; could once have been essential to our survival, and indeed still often is, for example when crossing the road. And involving the emotions in these anticipations could have been a smart idea. On the African savannah, our ancestors did not have the luxury of mulling over whether that screech was made by a harmless monkey or a predatory lion. By bypassing the &ldquo;logical brain&rdquo; and taking a shortcut to the primitive limbic circuits that control our emotions, the mental processing of sound could prompt a rush of adrenalin &ndash; a gut reaction &ndash; that prepares us to get out of there anyway.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;We all know that music has this direct line to the emotions: who hasn&rsquo;t been embarrassed by the tears that well up as the strings swell in a sentimental film, even while the logical brain protests that this is just cynical manipulation? We can&rsquo;t turn off this anticipatory instinct, nor its link to the emotions &ndash; even when we know that there&rsquo;s nothing life-threatening in a Mozart sonata. &ldquo;Nature&rsquo;s tendency to overreact provides a golden opportunity for musicians&rdquo;, says Huron. &ldquo;Composers can fashion passages that manage to provoke remarkably strong emotions using the most innocuous stimuli imaginable.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Sound check&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The idea that musical emotion arises from little violations and manipulations of our expectations seems the most promising candidate theory, but it is very hard to test. One reason for this is that music simply offers so much opportunity for creating and violating expectations that it&rsquo;s not clear what we should measure and compare. We expect rising melodies to continue to rise &ndash; but perhaps not indefinitely, as they never do. We expect pleasing harmonies rather than &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20130129-how-are-we-wired-for-sound\"&gt;jarring dissonance&lt;/a&gt; &ndash; but what sounds pleasing today may have seemed dissonant two hundred years ago. We expect rhythms to be regular, but are surprised if the jumpy syncopation of rock&rsquo;n&rsquo;roll suddenly switches to four-square oompah time. Expectation is a complicated, ever-changing interplay of how the piece we&rsquo;re hearing has gone so far, how it compares with similar pieces and styles, and how it compares with all we&rsquo;ve ever heard.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So, one corollary of Meyer&rsquo;s theory is that emotion in music will be primarily culturally specific. In order to have any expectations about where the music will go in the first place, you need to know the rules &ndash; to appreciate what is normal. This varies from one culture to another. Western Europeans think simple rhythms like waltz time are &ldquo;natural&rdquo;, but Eastern Europeans dance happily to metres that sound extraordinarily complicated to others. All of us develop a strong, subconscious sense of which notes sound &ldquo;right&rdquo;, whether in sequence in a melody, or sounding together in harmonies. But because different cultures use different scales and tunings &ndash; the scales of India and Indonesia, for example, don&rsquo;t respect the tunings of a piano &ndash; there is nothing universal about these expectations. A jolly piece of Indonesian music may be interpreted as &ldquo;sad&rdquo; by Westerners simply because it sounds close to being in the traditionally &ldquo;sad&rdquo; minor scale.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This picture also implies that music isn&rsquo;t just about good vibrations &ndash; it can provoke other feelings too, such as anxiety, boredom and even anger. Composers and performers walk a delicate tightrope, needing to tweak expectations to just the right degree. Not enough, and the music is dully predictable, as nursery tunes seem to adults. Too much, and we can&rsquo;t develop any expectations at all &ndash; which is why many people struggle with modernist atonal music.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;All this can rationalise a great deal about why we feel emotions from particular musical phrases and performances. Meyer&rsquo;s ideas have received further support very recently from a &lt;a href=\"http://www.sciencemag.org/content/340/6129/216.abstract\"&gt;brain-scanning study&lt;/a&gt; by Zatorre and colleagues, which showed that the rewards stimulated by music heard for the first time are particularly dependent on communication between &ldquo;emotion&rdquo; and &ldquo;logic&rdquo; circuits in the brain.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But it&rsquo;s not the whole story. Our emotional response to music may be conditioned by so many other factors too &ndash; if we are hearing it alone or in a crowd, for example, or if we associate a particular piece with a past experience, good or bad (dubbed the &ldquo;Darling they&rsquo;re playing our tune&rdquo; theory).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Underneath all these ideas is the fact that we&rsquo;re not even sure what kind of emotion we&rsquo;re talking about. We can recognise sad music without feeling sad. And even if we do feel sad, it&rsquo;s not like the sadness of bereavement &ndash; it can be enjoyable even if it provokes tears. Some music, like some of Bach&rsquo;s, can create intense emotion even though we can&rsquo;t quite put into words what the emotion is. So we&rsquo;ll surely never understand why music stimulates emotions at least until we have a better picture of what our emotional world is really like.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-04-19T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Will we ever understand why music makes us feel good?","HeadlineShort":"Why does music make us feel good?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"No one knows why music has such a potent effect on our emotions.
