body tag","LinkUrl":"http://dbpedialite.org/things/54176","Name":"Human body","CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:32Z","Entity":"tag","Guid":"40c347df-004b-4b1f-ad56-d1e7cf368548","Id":"tag/humanbody","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:32Z","Project":"","Slug":"humanbody"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:32Z","Entity":"tag","Guid":"40c347df-004b-4b1f-ad56-d1e7cf368548","Id":"tag/humanbody","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:32Z","Project":"","Slug":"humanbody"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:tag:tag/humanbody","_id":"559a57790b808738c4e31e7f"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":null,"Description":"Biology tag","LinkUrl":"http://dbpedialite.org/things/9127632","Name":"Biology","CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:50Z","Entity":"tag","Guid":"a40cafa3-4ad3-457e-88cc-3a9d36507fa4","Id":"tag/biology","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:50Z","Project":"","Slug":"biology"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:50Z","Entity":"tag","Guid":"a40cafa3-4ad3-457e-88cc-3a9d36507fa4","Id":"tag/biology","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:50Z","Project":"","Slug":"biology"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:tag:tag/biology","_id":"559a57760b808738c4e31e1c"}],"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:19Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"c0ba6221-5c65-47a8-9c57-86ef1c4cc1d2","Id":"wwfuture/story/20130717-what-makes-someone-an-extrovert","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:19Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20130717-what-makes-someone-an-extrovert"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:19Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"c0ba6221-5c65-47a8-9c57-86ef1c4cc1d2","Id":"wwfuture/story/20130717-what-makes-someone-an-extrovert","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:19Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20130717-what-makes-someone-an-extrovert"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20130717-what-makes-someone-an-extrovert","_id":"559a56170b808738c4e2f40f"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[{"Content":{"FileSizeBytes":32081,"MimeType":"image/jpeg","SourceHeight":315,"SourceUrl":"http://deltaorigin.bbc.co.uk/images/live/p0/1h/dl/p01hdl77.jpg","SourceWidth":560,"SynopsisLong":"(Copyright: Thinkstock)","SynopsisMedium":"(Copyright: Thinkstock)","SynopsisShort":"Does non-belief in free will make us better or worse?","TemplateUrl":"http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/$recipe/images/live/p0/1h/dl/p01hdl77.jpg","Title":"IT2_thinkstock.jpg","CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p01hdl77","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p01hdl77","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p01hdl77","_id":"559a541c0b808738c4e2a9d8"}],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Description":"&lt;p&gt;Tom is a Lecturer in Psychology and Cognitive Science for the Department\nof Psychology, University of Sheffield, UK. He is the co-author of the bestselling\npopular science book Mind Hacks and writes for the award-winning blog &lt;a href=\"http://mindhacks.com/\"&gt;Mind Hacks&lt;/a&gt; which reports on psychology and\nneuroscience. You can follow him on Twitter at &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/tomstafford\"&gt;@tomstafford&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;","Email":[],"Links":null,"Name":"Tom Stafford","PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:07Z","Entity":"author","Guid":"de5c2328-ffca-4de5-bb50-c350baf11d94","Id":"wwfuture/author/tom-stafford","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:07Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"tom-stafford"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:author:wwfuture/author/tom-stafford","_id":"559a524f0b808738c4e25fd4"}],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Are you reading this because you chose to? Or are you doing so as a result of forces beyond your control?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;After thousands of years of philosophy, theology, argument and meditation on the riddle of free will, I'm not about to solve it for you in this column (sorry). But what I can do is tell you about some thought-provoking experiments by psychologists, which suggest that, regardless of whether we have free will or not, whether we believe we do can have a profound impact on how we behave.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The issue is simple: we all make choices, but could those choices be made otherwise? From a religious perspective it might seem as if a divine being knows all, including knowing in advance what you will choose (so your choices could not be otherwise). Or we can take a physics-based perspective. Everything in the universe has physical causes, and as you are part of the universe, your choices must be caused (so your choices could not be otherwise). In either case, our experience of choosing collides with our faith in a world which makes sense because things have causes.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Consider for a moment how you would research whether a belief in free will affects our behaviour. There's no point comparing the behaviour of people with different fixed philosophical perspectives. You might find that determinists, who believe free will is an illusion and that we are all cogs in a godless universe, behave worse than those who believe we are free to make choices. But you wouldn't know whether this was simply because people who like to cheat and lie become determinists (the \"Yes, I lied, but I couldn't help it\" excuse).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;What we really need is a way of changing people&rsquo;s beliefs about free will, so that we can track the effects of doing so on their behaviour. Fortunately, in recent years researchers have developed a standard method of doing this. It involves asking subjects to read sections from Francis Crick's book The Astonishing Hypothesis. Crick was one of the co-discoverers of DNA&rsquo;s double-helix structure, for which he was awarded the Nobel prize. Later in his career he left molecular biology and devoted himself to neuroscience. The hypothesis in question is his belief that our mental life is entirely generated by the physical stuff of the brain. One passage states that neuroscience has killed the idea of free will, an idea that most rational people, including most scientists, now believe is an illusion.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Psychologists have used this section of the book, or sentences taken from it or inspired by it, to induce feelings of determinism in experimental subjects. A typical study asks people to read and think about a series of sentences such as \"Science has demonstrated that free will is an illusion&rdquo;, or \"Like everything else in the universe, all human actions follow from prior events and ultimately can be understood in terms of the movement of molecules&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The effects on study participants are generally compared with those of other people asked to read sentences that assert the existence of free will, such as \"I have feelings of regret when I make bad decisions because I know that ultimately I am responsible for my actions\", or texts on topics unrelated to free will.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And the results are striking. One study reported that participants who had their belief in free will diminished were more likely &lt;a href=\"http://www.csom.umn.edu/assets/91974.pdf\"&gt;to cheat in a maths test&lt;/a&gt;. In another, US psychologists reported that people who read Crick&rsquo;s thoughts on free will said they were &lt;a href=\"http://psp.sagepub.com/content/35/2/260.short\"&gt;less likely to help others&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Bad taste&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;A follow-up to this study used an ingenious method to test this via aggression to strangers. Participants were told a cover story about helping the experimenter prepare food for a taste test to be taken by a stranger. They were given the results of a supposed food preference questionnaire which indicated that the stranger liked most foods but hated hot food. Participants were also given a jar of hot sauce. The critical measure was how much of the sauce they put into the taste-test food. Putting in less sauce, when they knew that the taster didn't like hot food, meant they scored more highly for what psychologists call \"prosociality\", or what everyone else calls being nice.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;You've guessed it: Participants who had been reading about how they didn't have any free will chose to give more hot sauce to the poor fictional taster &ndash; twice as much, in fact, as those who read sentences supporting the idea of freedom of choice and responsibility.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In &lt;a href=\"http://pss.sagepub.com/content/22/5/613.short\"&gt;a recent study&lt;/a&gt; carried out at the University of Padova, Italy, researchers recorded the brain activity of participants who had been told to press a button whenever they wanted. This showed that people whose belief in free will had taken a battering thanks to reading Crick's views showed a weaker signal in areas of the brain involved in preparing to move. In another study by the same team, volunteers carried out a series of on-screen tasks designed to test their reaction times, self control and judgement. Those told free will didn't exist were slower, and more likely to go for &lt;a href=\"http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1053810012001171\"&gt;easier and more automatic courses of action&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This is a young research area. We still need to check that &lt;a href=\"http://rolfzwaan.blogspot.nl/2013/03/the-value-of-believing-in-free-will.html\"&gt;individual results hold up&lt;/a&gt;, but taken all together these studies show that our belief in free will isn't just a philosophical abstraction. We are less likely to behave ethically and kindly if our belief in free will is diminished.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This puts an extra burden of responsibility on philosophers, scientists, pundits and journalists who use evidence from psychology or neuroscience experiments to argue that free will is an illusion. We need to be careful about what stories we tell, given what we know about the likely consequences.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Fortunately, the evidence shows that most people have a sense of their individual freedom and responsibility that is &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/23833053?dopt=Abstract\"&gt;resistant to being overturned by neuroscience&lt;/a&gt;. Those sentences from Crick's book claim that most scientists believe free will to be an illusion. My guess is that most scientists would want to define what exactly is meant by free will, and to examine the &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elbow_Room_%28book%29\"&gt;various versions of free will on offer&lt;/a&gt;, before they agree whether it is an illusion or not.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;If the last few thousands of years have taught us anything, the debate about free will may rumble on and on. But whether the outcome is inevitable or not, these results show that how we think about the way we think could have a profound effect on us, and on others.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook page&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":null,"CollectionType":"column","Description":"Neuroscience and the psychology of the everyday. Psychologist Tom Stafford explores the surprisingly complex theories behind experiences we all take for granted.","Name":"Neurohacks","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Neurohacks","CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:56.413888Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fcdb0306-a2e2-447c-ae2c-cf60ee943f26","Id":"wwfuture/column/neurohacks","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:56.413888Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"column/neurohacks"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:56.413888Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fcdb0306-a2e2-447c-ae2c-cf60ee943f26","Id":"wwfuture/column/neurohacks","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:56.413888Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"column/neurohacks"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/column/neurohacks","_id":"559a52610b808738c4e262a9"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Health","Name":"Health","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Health"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:44:06.503434Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"00a9a33a-e393-4d09-8029-7e9d5bd1d8cb","Id":"wwfuture/section/health","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:52:37.074442Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/health"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/health","_id":"559a52620b808738c4e262db"}],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-09-25T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Does non-belief in free will make us better or worse?","HeadlineShort":"How belief in free will shapes us","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Studies have shown that people who believe things happen randomly and not through our own choice often behave much worse than those who believe the opposite.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;We're taught from childhood how important it is to explain how we feel and to always justify our actions. But does giving reasons always make things clearer, or could it sometimes distract us from our true feelings?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One answer came from a study led by &lt;a href=\"http://psp.sagepub.com/content/19/3/331.abstract\"&gt;psychology professor Timothy Wilson&lt;/a&gt; at the University of Virginia, which asked university students to report their feelings, either with or without being asked to provide reasons. What they found revealed just how difficult it can be to reliably discern our feelings when justifying our decisions.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Participants were asked to evaluate five posters of the kind that students might put up in their bedrooms. Two of the posters were of art &ndash; one was Monet's water lilies, the other Van Gogh's irises. The other three posters were a cartoon of animals in a balloon and two posters of photographs of cats with funny captions.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;All the students had to evaluate the posters, but half the participants were asked to provide reasons for liking or disliking them. (The other half were asked why they chose their degree subject as a control condition.) After they had provided their evaluations the participants were allowed to choose a poster to take home.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So what happened? The control group rated the art posters positively (an average score of around 7 out of 9) and they felt pretty neutral about the humorous posters (an average score of around 4 out of 9). When given a choice of one poster to take home, 95% of them chose one of the art posters. No surprises there, the experimenters had already established that in general most students preferred the art posters.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But the group of students who had to give reasons for their feelings acted differently. This &ldquo;reasons&rdquo; group liked the art posters less (averaging about 6 out of 9) and the humorous posters more (about 5 to 6 out of 9). Most of them still chose an art poster to take home, but it was a far lower proportion &ndash; 64% &ndash; than the control group. That means people in this group were about seven times more likely to take a humorous poster home compared with the control group.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Here&rsquo;s the twist. Some time after the tests, at the end of the semester, the researchers rang each of the participants and asked them questions about the poster they'd chosen: Had they put it up in their room? Did they still have it? How did they feel about it? How much would they be willing to sell it for? The &ldquo;reasons&rdquo; group were less likely to have put their poster up, less likely to have kept it up, less satisfied with it on average and were willing to part with it for a smaller average amount than the control group. Over time their reasons and feelings had shifted back in line with those of the control group &ndash; they didn't like the humorous posters they had taken home, and so were less happy about their choice.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Trivial pursuit&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The source of this effect, according to the researchers, is that when prompted to give reasons the participants focused on things that were easy to verbalise; they focused on the bright colours, or funny content of the humorous posters. It's less easy to say exactly what's pleasing about the more complex art classics. This was out of step with their feelings, so in the heat of the moment participants adjusted their feelings (a process I've written about before, called &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20130312-why-we-act-against-our-beliefs\"&gt;cognitive dissonance&lt;/a&gt;). After having the posters on their wall, the participants realised that they really did prefer the art posters all along.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The moral of the story isn&rsquo;t that intuition is better than reason. We all know that in some situations our feelings are misleading and it is better to think about what we're doing. But this study shows the reverse &ndash; in some situations introspection can interfere with using our feelings as a reliable guide to what we should do.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And this has consequences in adulthood, where the notion of expertise can mean struggling to discern when introspection is the best strategy. The researchers who carried out this study suggest that the distorting effect of reason-giving is most likely to occur in situations where people aren't experts &ndash; most of the students who took part in the study didn't have a lot of experience of thinking or talking about art. When experts are asked to give reasons for their feelings, research has found that their feelings aren&rsquo;t &lt;a href=\"http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/0022103189900292\"&gt;distorted in the same way&lt;/a&gt; &ndash; their intuitions and explicit reasoning are in sync.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;You might also see the consequences of this regularly in your line of work. Everybody knows that the average business meeting will spend the most time discussing &lt;a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parkinson%27s_law_of_triviality\"&gt;trivial things&lt;/a&gt;, an effect driven by the ease with which each member of the meeting can chip in about something as inconsequential as what colour to paint the bike sheds. When we're discussing complex issues, it isn't so easy to make a contribution. The danger, of course, is that in a world which relies on justification and measurement of everything, those things that are most easily justified and measured will get priority over those things which are, in fact, most justified and important.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this story or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-06-05T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Why we dont always know the true causes of our actions","HeadlineShort":"Why your gut feeling may be wrong","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"We can justify our decisions by how we feel, or so we believe. The problem, as psychology studies have shown, is that we often make up these reasons.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"We can justify our decisions by how we feel, or so we believe. The problem, as psychology studies have shown, is that we often make up these reasons.","SummaryShort":"Psychology behind justifying our actions ","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:48Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"6d42d023-e217-4ac8-97f8-af46ca554f88","Id":"wwfuture/story/20130605-why-not-to-trust-your-gut-feeling","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:48Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20130605-why-not-to-trust-your-gut-feeling"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20130605-why-not-to-trust-your-gut-feeling","_id":"559a56130b808738c4e2f38f"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;If at first you don't succeed, lower your standards. And if you find yourself acting out of line with your beliefs, change them. This sounds like motivational advice from one of the more cynical self-help books, or perhaps a Groucho Marx line (&ldquo;Those are my principles, and if you don't like them... well, I have others&hellip;&rdquo;), but in fact it is a caricature of one of the most famous theories in social psychology.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Leon Festinger's Dissonance Theory is an account of how our beliefs rub up against each other, an attempt at a sort of ecology of mind. Dissonance Theory offers an explanation of topics as diverse as why oil company executives might not believe in climate change, why army units have brutal initiation ceremonies, and why famous books might actually be boring.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The classic study on dissonance theory was published by Festinger and James Carlsmith in 1959. You can find a copy thanks to the &lt;a href=\"http://psychclassics.yorku.ca/Festinger/index.htm\"&gt;Classics in the History of Psychology archive&lt;/a&gt;. I really recommend reading the full thing. Not only is it short, but it is full of enjoyable asides. Back in the day psychology research was a lot more fun to write up.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Festinger and Carlsmith were interested in testing what happened when people acted out of line with their beliefs. To do this, they made their participants spend an hour doing two excruciatingly boring tasks. The first task was filling a tray with spools, emptying it, then filling it again (and so on). The second was turning 48 small pegs a quarter-turn clockwise; and then once that was finished, going back to the beginning and doing another quarter-turn for each peg (and so on). Only after this tedium, and at the point which the participants believed the experiment was over, did the real study get going. The experimenter said that they needed someone to fill in at the last minute and explain the tasks to the next subject. Would they mind? And also, could they make the points that \"It was very enjoyable&rdquo;, &ldquo;I had a lot of fun&rdquo;, &ldquo;I enjoyed myself&rdquo;, &ldquo;It was very interesting&rdquo;, &ldquo;It was intriguing&rdquo;, and &ldquo;It was exciting\"?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Of course the &ldquo;experiment&rdquo; was none of these things. But, being good people, with some pleading if necessary, they all agreed to explain the experiment to the next participant and make these points. The next participant was, of course, a confederate of the experimenter. We're not told much about her, except that she was an undergraduate specifically hired for the role. The fact that all 71 participants in the experiment were male, and, that one of the 71 had to be excluded from the final analysis because he demanded her phone number so he could explain things further, suggests that Festinger and Carlsmith weren't above ensuring that there were some extra motivational factors in the mix.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Money trap&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For their trouble, the participants were paid $1, $20, or nothing. After explaining the task the original participants answered some questions about how they really felt about the experiment. At the time, many psychologists would have predicted that the group paid the most would be affected the most &ndash; if our feelings are shaped by rewards, the people paid $20 should be the ones who said they enjoyed it the most.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In fact, people paid $20 tended to feel the same about the experiment as the people paid nothing. But something strange happened with the people paid $1. These participants were more likely to say they really did find the experiment enjoyable. They judged the experiment as more important scientifically, and had the highest desire to participate in future similar experiments. Which is weird, since nobody should really want to spend another hour doing mundane, repetitive tasks.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Festinger's Dissonance theory explains the result. The &ldquo;Dissonance&rdquo; is between the actions of the participants and their beliefs about themselves. Here they are, nice guys, lying to an innocent woman. Admittedly there are lots of other social forces at work &ndash; obligation, authority, even attraction. Festinger's interpretation is that these things may play a role in how the participants act, but they can't be explicitly relied upon as reasons for acting. So there is a tension between their belief that they are a nice person and the knowledge of how they acted. This is where the cash payment comes in. People paid $20 have an easy rationalisation to hand. \"Sure, I lied\", they can say to themselves, \"but I did it for $20\". The men who got paid the smaller amount, $1, can't do this. Giving the money as a reason would make them look cheap, as well as mean. Instead, the story goes, they adjust their beliefs to be in line with how they acted. \"Sure, the experiment was kind of interesting, just like I told that girl\", \"It was fun, I wouldn't mind being in her position\" and so on.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So this is cognitive dissonance at work. Normally it should be a totally healthy process &ndash; after all, who could object to people being motivated to reduce contradictions in their beliefs (philosophers even make a profession of out this), but in circumstances where some of our actions or our beliefs exist for reasons which are too complex, too shameful, or too nebulous to articulate, it can lead to us changing perfectly valid beliefs, such as how boring and pointless a task was.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Fans of cognitive dissonance will tell you that this is why people forced to defend a particular position &ndash; say because it is their job &ndash; are likely to end up believing it. It can also suggest a reason for why military services, high school sports teams and college societies have bizarre and punishing initiation rituals. If you've been through the ritual, dissonance theory predicts, you're much more likely to believe the group is a valuable one to be a part of (the initiation hurt, and you're not a fool, so it must have been worth it right?).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For me, I think dissonance theory explains why some really long books have such good reputations, despite the fact that they may be as repetitive and pointless as Festinger's peg task. Get to the end of a three-volume, several thousand page, conceptual novel and you're faced with a choice: either you wasted your time and money, and you feel a bit of a fool; or the novel is brilliant and you are an insightful consumer of literature. Dissonance theory pushes you towards the latter interpretation, and so swells the crowd of people praising a novel that would be panned if it was 150 pages long.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Changing your beliefs to be in line with how you acted may not be the most principled approach. But it is certainly easier than changing how you acted.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-03-12T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Why people act out of line with their beliefs","HeadlineShort":"Why we act against our beliefs","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Ever been forced to defend a particular position and ended up believing it? Youve succumbed to one of the most famous theories in social psychology. ","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"Ever been forced to defend a particular position and ended up believing it? Youve succumbed to one of the most famous theories in social psychology. ","SummaryShort":"One of the most famous theories in psychology","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:36Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"ccb6f6e7-d67e-43cf-b43b-caf64d0875cd","Id":"wwfuture/story/20130312-why-we-act-against-our-beliefs","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:36Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20130312-why-we-act-against-our-beliefs"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20130312-why-we-act-against-our-beliefs","_id":"559a560a0b808738c4e2f27d"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Ouija board cups and dowsing wands &ndash; just two examples of mystical items that seem to move of their own accord, when they are really being moved by the people holding them. The only mystery is not one of a connection to the spirit world, but of why we can make movements and yet not realise that we're making them.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The phenomenon is called the &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ideomotor_effect\"&gt;ideomotor effect&lt;/a&gt; and you can witness it yourself if you hang a small weight like a button or a ring from a string (ideally more than a foot long). Hold the end of the string with your arm out in front of you, so the weight hangs down freely. Try to hold your arm completely still. The weight will start to swing clockwise or anticlockwise in small circles. Do not start this motion yourself. Instead, just ask yourself a question &ndash; any question &ndash; and say that the weight will swing clockwise to answer \"Yes\" and anticlockwise for \"No\". Hold this thought in mind, and soon, even though you are trying not to make any motion, the weight will start to swing in answer to your question.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Magic? Only the ordinary everyday magic of consciousness. There's no supernatural force at work, just tiny movements you are making without realising. The string allows these movements to be exaggerated, the inertia of the weight allows them to be conserved and built on until they form a regular swinging motion. The effect is known as Chevreul's Pendulum, after the 19th Century French scientist who investigated it.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;What is happening with Chevreul's Pendulum is that you are witnessing a movement (of the weight) without \"owning\" that movement as being caused by you. The same basic phenomenon underlies dowsing &ndash; where small movements of the hands cause the dowsing wand to swing wildly &ndash; or the Ouija board, where multiple people hold a cup and it seems to move of its own accord to answer questions by spelling out letters.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This effect also underlies the sad case of \"&lt;a href=\"http://www.skepdic.com/facilcom.html\"&gt;facilitated communication&lt;/a&gt;\", a fad whereby carers believed they could help severely disabled children communicate by guiding their fingers around a keyboard. Research showed that the carers &ndash; completely innocently &ndash; were typing the messages themselves, rather than interpreting movements from their charges.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The interesting thing about the phenomenon is what it says about the mind. That we can make movements that we don't realise we're making suggests that we shouldn't be so confident in our other judgements about what movements we think are ours. Sure enough, in the right circumstances, you can get people to believe they have caused things that actually come from a completely independent source (something which shouldn't surprise anyone who has reflected on the madness of people who claim that it only started raining because they forget an umbrella).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;You can read what this means for the nature of our minds in &lt;a href=\"http://www.wjh.harvard.edu/~wegner/conscwil.htm\"&gt;The Illusion of Conscious Will&lt;/a&gt; by psychologist Daniel Wegner, who sadly &lt;a href=\"http://www.boston.com/news/local/massachusetts/2013/07/11/daniel-wegner-harvard-social-psychologist-unraveled-mysteries-thought-and-memory/g1So09GTjK40ikR0Tyyi4H/singlepage.html\"&gt;died last month&lt;/a&gt;. Wegner argued that our normal sense of owning an action is an illusion, or &ndash; if you will &ndash; a construction. The mental processes which directly control our movements are not connected to the same processes which figure out what caused what, he claimed.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The situation is not that of a mental command-and-control structure like a disciplined army; whereby a general issues orders to the troops, they carry out the order and the general gets back a report saying \"Sir! We did it. The right hand is moving into action!\". The situation is more akin to an organised collective, claims Wegner: the general can issue orders, and watch what happens, but he's never sure exactly what caused what. Instead, just like with other people, our consciousness (the general in this metaphor) has to apply some principles to figure out when a movement is one we've made.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One of these principles is that cause has to be consistent with effect. If you think \"I'll move my hand\" and your hand moves, you're likely to automatically get the feeling that the movement was one you made. The principle is broken when the thought is different from the effect, such as with Chevreul's Pendulum. If you think \"I'm not moving my hand\", you are less inclined to connect any small movements you make with such large visual effects.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This maybe explains why kids can shout \"It wasn't me!\" after breaking something in plain sight. They thought to themselves \"I'll just give this a little push\", and when it falls off the table and breaks it doesn't feel like something they did.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-07-30T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"How the ouija board really moves","HeadlineShort":"How the ouija board really moves","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"The mystery isnt a connection to the spirit world, but why we can make movements and yet not realise that we're making them.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"The mystery isnt a connection to the spirit world, but why we can make movements and yet not realise that we're making them.","SummaryShort":"Clue: its you... but how does it happen?","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:15:15Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"5addc37b-85f4-4f0e-8fd5-18209123aa0d","Id":"wwfuture/story/20130729-what-makes-the-ouija-board-move","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:15:15Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20130729-what-makes-the-ouija-board-move"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20130729-what-makes-the-ouija-board-move","_id":"559a56180b808738c4e2f431"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;One of the least charming but most persistent aspects of human nature is our capacity to hate people who are different. Racism, sexism, ageism, it seems like all the major social categories come with their own &ldquo;-ism&rdquo;, each fuelled by regrettable prejudice and bigotry.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Our tendency for groupness appears to be so strong there seems little more for psychology to teach us. It's not as if we need it proven that favouring our group over others is a common part of how people think &ndash; history provides all the examples we need. But one psychologist, &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Henri_tajfel\"&gt;Henri Tajfel&lt;/a&gt;, taught us something important. He showed exactly how little encouragement we need to treat people in a biased way because of the group they are in.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Any phenomenon like this in the real world comes entangled with a bunch of other, complicating phenomenon. When we see prejudice in the everyday world it is hard to separate out psychological biases from the effects of history, culture and even pragmatism (sometimes people from other groups really are out to get you).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As a social psychologist, Tajfel was interested in the essential conditions of group prejudice. He wanted to know what it took to turn the average fair-minded human into their prejudiced cousin.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;He wanted to create a microscope for looking at how we think when we&rsquo;re part of a group, even when that group has none of the history, culture or practical importance that groups normally do. To look at this, he devised what has become known as the &ldquo;&lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minimal_group_paradigm\"&gt;minimal group paradigm&lt;/a&gt;&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The minimal group paradigm works like this: participants in the experiment are divided into groups on some arbitrary basis. Maybe eye-colour, maybe what kind of paintings they like, or even by tossing a coin. It doesn't matter what the basis for group membership is, as long as everyone gets a group and knows what it is. After being told they are in a group, participants are divided up so that they are alone when they make a series of choices about how rewards will be shared among other people in the groups. From this point on, group membership is entirely abstract. Nobody else can be seen, and other group members are referred to by an anonymous number. Participants make choices such as \"Member Number 74 (group A) to get 10 points and Member 44 (group B) to get 8 points\", versus \"Member Number 74 (group A) to get 2 points and Member 44 (group B) to get 6 points\", where the numbers are points which translate into real money.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;You won't be surprised to learn that &lt;a href=\"http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1002/ejsp.2420010202/full\"&gt;participants show favouritism&lt;/a&gt; towards their own group when dividing the money. People in group A were more likely to choose the first option I gave above, rather than the second. What is more surprising is that people show some of this group favouritism even when it ends up costing them points &ndash; so people in group B sometimes choose the second option, or options like it, even though it provides fewer points than the first option. People tend to opt for the maximum total reward (as you'd expect from the fair-minded citizen), but they also show a tendency to maximise the difference between the groups (what you'd expect from the prejudiced cousin).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The effect may be small, but this is a situation where the groups have been plucked out of the air by the experimenters. Every participant knows which group he or she is in, but they also know that they weren't in this group before they started the experiment, that their assignment was arbitrary or completely random, and that the groups aren't going to exist in any meaningful way after the experiment. They also know that their choices won't directly affect them (they are explicitly told that they won't be given any choices to make about themselves). Even so, this situation is enough to evoke favouritism.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So, it seems we'll take the most minimal of signs as a cue to treat people differently according to which group they are in. Tajfel's work suggests that in-group bias is as fundamental to thinking as the act of categorisations itself. If we want to contribute to a fairer world we need to be perpetually on guard to avoid letting this instinct run away with itself.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Do you agree with Tom? Surprisingly easy, according to psychology studies.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"How easy is it for the average fair-minded person to form biased, preconceived views within groups? Surprisingly easy, according to psychology studies.","SummaryShort":"Little encouragement, say psychologists.","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:40Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"d8d8d276-215a-4f54-bac5-95c5b1883efb","Id":"wwfuture/story/20130409-what-sparks-prejudice-in-humans","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:40Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20130409-what-sparks-prejudice-in-humans"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20130409-what-sparks-prejudice-in-humans","_id":"559a560d0b808738c4e2f2e5"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":[],"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;In Tim Lewis&rsquo;s world, bizarre kinetic sculptures move, flap wings, draw and &lt;a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ZJ-nrqzvX7Q\"&gt;even walk around&lt;/a&gt;. The British artist creates mechanical animals and animal machines - like &lt;a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=L0sbXNdNEfQ\"&gt;Pony&lt;/a&gt;, a robotic ostrich with an arm for a neck and a poised hand for a head &ndash; that creak into life in a way that can seem unsettling, as if they have a strange, if awkward, life of their own. His latest creations are able to respond to the environment, and it makes me ponder the essence of intelligence &ndash; in some ways revealing what makes us, us.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;I met Tim on a cold Friday afternoon to talk about his work, and while talking about the cogs and gears he uses to make his artwork move, he made a remark that made me stop in my tracks. The funny thing is, he said, all of the technology existed to make machines like this in the sixteenth century - the thing that stopped them wasn't the technical know-how, it was because they lacked the right model of the mind.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;What model of the mind do you need to create a device like Tim's &lt;a href=\"http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=L0sbXNdNEfQ\"&gt;Jetsam&lt;/a&gt;, a large wire mesh Kiwi-like creature that forages around its cage for pieces of a nest to build. The intelligence in this creation isn't in the precision of the craftwork (although it is precise), or in the faithfulness to the kind of movements seen in nature (although it is faithful). The intelligence is in how it responds to the placing of the sticks. It isn't programmed in advance, it identifies where each piece is and where it needs to go&lt;strong&gt;.&lt;/strong&gt;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This gives Jetsam the hallmark of intelligence &ndash; flexibility. If the environment changes, say when the sticks are re-scattered at random, it can still adapt and find the materials to build its nest. Rather than a brain giving instructions such as \"Do this\", feedback allows instructions such as \"If this, do that; if that, do the other\". Crucially, feedback allows a machine to follow a purpose &ndash; if the goal changes, the machine can adapt. &lt;br /&gt; &nbsp;&lt;br /&gt; It&rsquo;s this quality that the sixteenth century clockwork models lacked, and one that we as humans almost take for granted. We grasp and process information about the world in many forms, including sights, smells or sounds. We may give these information sources different names, but in some sense, these are essentially the same stuff.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Information control&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cybernetics\"&gt;Cybernetics&lt;/a&gt; is the name given to the study of feedback, and systems that use feedback, in all their forms. The term comes from the Greek word for \"to steer\", and inspiration for some of the early work on cybernetics sprang from automatic guiding systems developed during World War II for guns or radar antennae. Around the middle of the twentieth century cybernetics became an intellectual movement across many different disciplines. It created a common language that allowed engineers to talk with psychologists, or ecologists to talk to mathematicians, about living organisms from the viewpoint of information control systems. &lt;br /&gt; &nbsp;&lt;br /&gt; A key message of cybernetics is that you can't control something unless you have feedback &ndash; and that means measurement of the outcomes. You can&rsquo;t hit a moving target unless you get feedback on changes to its movement, just as you can&rsquo;t tell if a drug is a cure unless you get feedback on how many more people recover when they are given it. The flip side of this dictum is the promise that with feedback, you can control anything. The human brain seems to be the arch embodiment of this cybernetic principle. With the right feedback, individuals have been known to control things as unlikely as their own &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/22744827?dopt=Abstract\"&gt;heart rate&lt;/a&gt;, or learn to &lt;a href=\"http://www.wikihow.com/Dilate-or-Shrink-Your-Pupils-on-Command\"&gt;shrink and expand their pupils at will&lt;/a&gt;. It even seems possible to control the &lt;a href=\"http://www.nature.com/nature/journal/v456/n7222/full/nature07418.html\"&gt;firing of individual brain cells&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But enhanced feedback methods can accelerate learning about more mundane behaviours. For example, if you are learning to take basketball shots, augmented feedback in the form of \"You were 3 inches off to the left\" can help you &lt;a href=\"http://www.tandfonline.com/doi/abs/10.1080/00336297.1994.10484129\"&gt;learn faster&lt;/a&gt; and reach a higher skill level quicker. Perhaps the most powerful example of an augmented feedback loop is the development of writing, which allowed us to take language and experiences, and make them permanent, solidifying it against the ravages of time, space and memory.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Thanks to feedback we can become more than simple programs with simple reflexes, and develop more complex responses to the environment. Feedback allows animals like us to follow a purpose. Tim Lewis's mechanical bird might seem simple, but in terms of intelligence it has more in common with us than with nearly all other machines that humans have built. Engines or clocks might be incredibly sophisticated, but until they are able to gather their own data about the environment they remain trapped in fixed patterns.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Feedback loops, on the other hand, beginning with the senses but extending out across time and many individuals, allow us to self-construct, letting us travel to places we don't have the instructions for beforehand, and letting us build on the history of our actions. In this way humanity pulls itself up by its own bootstraps.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook page&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-02-26T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Why your brain loves to get feedback","HeadlineShort":"Why your brain loves feedback","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"A meeting with an artist gets Tom Stafford thinking about the essence of intelligence. is a Lecturer in Psychology and Cognitive Science for the Department\nof Psychology, University of Sheffield, UK. He is the co-author of the bestselling\npopular science book Mind Hacks and writes for the award-winning blog &lt;a href=\"http://mindhacks.com/\"&gt;Mind Hacks&lt;/a&gt; which reports on psychology and\nneuroscience. You can follow him on Twitter at &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/tomstafford\"&gt;@tomstafford&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;","Email":[],"Links":null,"Name":"Tom Stafford","PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:07Z","Entity":"author","Guid":"de5c2328-ffca-4de5-bb50-c350baf11d94","Id":"wwfuture/author/tom-stafford","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:07Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"tom-stafford"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:author:wwfuture/author/tom-stafford","_id":"559a524f0b808738c4e25fd4"}],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;One of the least charming but most persistent aspects of human nature is our capacity to hate people who are different. Racism, sexism, ageism, it seems like all the major social categories come with their own &ldquo;-ism&rdquo;, each fuelled by regrettable prejudice and bigotry.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Our tendency for groupness appears to be so strong there seems little more for psychology to teach us. It's not as if we need it proven that favouring our group over others is a common part of how people think &ndash; history provides all the examples we need. But one psychologist, &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Henri_tajfel\"&gt;Henri Tajfel&lt;/a&gt;, taught us something important. He showed exactly how little encouragement we need to treat people in a biased way because of the group they are in.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Any phenomenon like this in the real world comes entangled with a bunch of other, complicating phenomenon. When we see prejudice in the everyday world it is hard to separate out psychological biases from the effects of history, culture and even pragmatism (sometimes people from other groups really are out to get you).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As a social psychologist, Tajfel was interested in the essential conditions of group prejudice. He wanted to know what it took to turn the average fair-minded human into their prejudiced cousin.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;He wanted to create a microscope for looking at how we think when we&rsquo;re part of a group, even when that group has none of the history, culture or practical importance that groups normally do. To look at this, he devised what has become known as the &ldquo;&lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minimal_group_paradigm\"&gt;minimal group paradigm&lt;/a&gt;&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The minimal group paradigm works like this: participants in the experiment are divided into groups on some arbitrary basis. Maybe eye-colour, maybe what kind of paintings they like, or even by tossing a coin. It doesn't matter what the basis for group membership is, as long as everyone gets a group and knows what it is. After being told they are in a group, participants are divided up so that they are alone when they make a series of choices about how rewards will be shared among other people in the groups. From this point on, group membership is entirely abstract. Nobody else can be seen, and other group members are referred to by an anonymous number. Participants make choices such as \"Member Number 74 (group A) to get 10 points and Member 44 (group B) to get 8 points\", versus \"Member Number 74 (group A) to get 2 points and Member 44 (group B) to get 6 points\", where the numbers are points which translate into real money.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;You won't be surprised to learn that &lt;a href=\"http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1002/ejsp.2420010202/full\"&gt;participants show favouritism&lt;/a&gt; towards their own group when dividing the money. People in group A were more likely to choose the first option I gave above, rather than the second. What is more surprising is that people show some of this group favouritism even when it ends up costing them points &ndash; so people in group B sometimes choose the second option, or options like it, even though it provides fewer points than the first option. People tend to opt for the maximum total reward (as you'd expect from the fair-minded citizen), but they also show a tendency to maximise the difference between the groups (what you'd expect from the prejudiced cousin).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The effect may be small, but this is a situation where the groups have been plucked out of the air by the experimenters. Every participant knows which group he or she is in, but they also know that they weren't in this group before they started the experiment, that their assignment was arbitrary or completely random, and that the groups aren't going to exist in any meaningful way after the experiment. They also know that their choices won't directly affect them (they are explicitly told that they won't be given any choices to make about themselves). Even so, this situation is enough to evoke favouritism.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So, it seems we'll take the most minimal of signs as a cue to treat people differently according to which group they are in. Tajfel's work suggests that in-group bias is as fundamental to thinking as the act of categorisations itself. If we want to contribute to a fairer world we need to be perpetually on guard to avoid letting this instinct run away with itself.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Do you agree with Tom?
